			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Solar power</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">This article is about generation of electricity using solar energy. For other uses of solar energy, see <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">Solar energy</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Solar_luminosity" title="Solar luminosity">Solar luminosity</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px">

</div>

</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">

<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Average insolation. Note that this is for a horizontal surface, whereas solar panels are normally propped up at an angle and receive more energy per unit area, especially at high latitudes. Potential of <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar energy</a>. The small black dots show land area required to replace the world <a href="/wiki/Primary_energy" title="Primary energy">primary energy</a> supply with solar power.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Solar power</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Energy_transformation" title="Energy transformation">conversion of energy</a> from <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> into <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>, either directly using <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">photovoltaics</a> (PV), or indirectly using <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">concentrated solar power</a>. Concentrated solar power systems use <a href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">lenses</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mirrors" class="mw-redirect" title="Mirrors">mirrors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_tracking" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar tracking">tracking systems</a> to focus a large area of sunlight into a small beam. Photovoltaic cells convert light into an <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a> using the <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_effect" title="Photovoltaic effect">photovoltaic effect</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> projected in 2014 that under its "high renewables" scenario, by 2050, solar photovoltaics and concentrated solar power would contribute about 16 and 11 percent, respectively, of the <a href="/wiki/Worldwide_electricity_consumption" class="mw-redirect" title="Worldwide electricity consumption">worldwide electricity consumption</a>, and solar would be the world's largest source of electricity. Most solar installations would be in <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_China" title="Solar power in China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_India" title="Solar power in India">India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Photovoltaics were initially solely used as a source of <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> for small and medium-sized applications, from the <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_silicon#Solar_cells" title="Amorphous silicon">calculator</a> powered by a single solar cell to remote homes powered by an <a href="/wiki/Off-grid" class="mw-redirect" title="Off-grid">off-grid</a> rooftop PV system. As the cost of solar electricity has fallen, the number of grid-connected <a href="/wiki/Solar_PV_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar PV systems">solar PV systems</a> has <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_photovoltaics" title="Growth of photovoltaics">grown into the millions</a> and utility-scale <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_power_station" title="Photovoltaic power station">solar power stations</a> with hundreds of megawatts are being built. Solar PV is rapidly becoming an inexpensive, low-carbon technology to harness <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> from the Sun. The current largest photovoltaic power station in the world is the 850&#160;MW <a href="/wiki/Longyangxia_Dam" title="Longyangxia Dam">Longyangxia Dam</a> Solar Park, in <a href="/wiki/Qinghai" title="Qinghai">Qinghai</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.</p>
<p>Commercial concentrated solar power plants were first developed in the 1980s. The 392&#160;MW <a href="/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility" title="Ivanpah Solar Power Facility">Ivanpah</a> installation is the largest concentrating solar power plant in the world, located in the <a href="/wiki/Mojave_Desert" title="Mojave Desert">Mojave Desert</a> of <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mainstream_technologies">Mainstream technologies</span></h2>
<p>Many industrialized nations have installed significant solar power capacity into their grids to supplement or provide an alternative to <a href="/wiki/Conventional_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Conventional energy">conventional energy</a> sources while an increasing number of less developed nations have turned to solar to reduce dependence on expensive imported fuels <i>(see <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_by_country" title="Solar power by country">solar power by country</a>)</i>. Long distance transmission allows remote <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> resources to displace fossil fuel consumption. Solar power plants use one of two technologies:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Photovoltaic">Photovoltaic</a> (PV) <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Photovoltaic systems">systems</a> use <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a>, either on <a href="/wiki/Rooftop_photovoltaic_power_station" title="Rooftop photovoltaic power station">rooftops</a> or in ground-mounted <a href="/wiki/Solar_farm" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar farm">solar farms</a>, converting sunlight directly into electric power.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">Concentrated solar power</a> (CSP, also known as "concentrated solar thermal") plants use <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">solar thermal energy</a> to make steam, that is thereafter converted into electricity by a turbine.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photovoltaics">Photovoltaics</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">Photovoltaics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:247px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png/245px-PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png" width="245" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png/368px-PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png/490px-PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png 2x" data-file-width="975" data-file-height="567" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Schematics of a grid-connected residential <a href="/wiki/PV_power_system" class="mw-redirect" title="PV power system">PV power system</a><sup id="cite_ref-SolarCells_Section10_2_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SolarCells_Section10_2-3">[3]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cell</a>, or photovoltaic cell (PV), is a device that converts light into electric current using the <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_effect" title="Photovoltaic effect">photovoltaic effect</a>. The first solar cell was constructed by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Fritts" title="Charles Fritts">Charles Fritts</a> in the 1880s.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The German industrialist <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Werner_von_Siemens" class="mw-redirect" title="Ernst Werner von Siemens">Ernst Werner von Siemens</a> was among those who recognized the importance of this discovery.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> In 1931, the German engineer Bruno Lange developed a photo cell using <a href="/wiki/Silver_selenide" class="mw-redirect" title="Silver selenide">silver selenide</a> in place of <a href="/wiki/Copper_oxide" title="Copper oxide">copper oxide</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> although the prototype <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a> cells converted less than 1% of incident light into electricity. Following the work of <a href="/wiki/Russell_Ohl" title="Russell Ohl">Russell Ohl</a> in the 1940s, researchers Gerald Pearson, <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Fuller" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvin Fuller">Calvin Fuller</a> and Daryl Chapin created the <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> solar cell in 1954.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> These early solar cells cost 286&#160;USD/watt and reached efficiencies of 4.5–6%.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conventional_PV_systems">Conventional PV systems</span></h4>
<p>The array of a <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_power_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Photovoltaic power system">photovoltaic power system</a>, or PV system, produces direct current (DC) power which fluctuates with the sunlight's intensity. For practical use this usually requires conversion to certain desired voltages or alternating current (AC), through the use of <a href="/wiki/Solar_inverter" title="Solar inverter">inverters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SolarCells_Section10_2_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SolarCells_Section10_2-3">[3]</a></sup> Multiple solar cells are connected inside modules. Modules are wired together to form arrays, then tied to an inverter, which produces power at the desired voltage, and for AC, the desired frequency/phase.<sup id="cite_ref-SolarCells_Section10_2_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SolarCells_Section10_2-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many residential PV systems are connected to the grid wherever available, especially in developed countries with large markets.<sup id="cite_ref-IEAPVPS2009_Fig3_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEAPVPS2009_Fig3-9">[9]</a></sup> In these <a href="/wiki/Grid-connected_photovoltaic_power_system" title="Grid-connected photovoltaic power system">grid-connected PV systems</a>, use of energy storage is optional. In certain applications such as satellites, lighthouses, or in developing countries, batteries or additional power generators are often added as back-ups. Such <a href="/wiki/Stand-alone_power_system" title="Stand-alone power system">stand-alone power systems</a> permit operations at night and at other times of limited sunlight.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concentrated_solar_power">Concentrated solar power</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">Concentrated solar power</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Parabolic_trough.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Parabolic_trough.svg/170px-Parabolic_trough.svg.png" width="170" height="340" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Parabolic_trough.svg/255px-Parabolic_trough.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Parabolic_trough.svg/340px-Parabolic_trough.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Parabolic_trough.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_trough" title="Parabolic trough">parabolic collector</a> concentrates sunlight onto a tube in its focal point.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Concentrated solar power (CSP), also called "concentrated solar thermal", uses lenses or mirrors and tracking systems to focus a large area of sunlight into a small beam. Contrary to photovoltaics – which converts light directly into electricity – CSP uses the heat of the sun's radiation to generate electricity from conventional steam-driven turbines.</p>
<p>A wide range of concentrating technologies exists: among the best known are the <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_trough" title="Parabolic trough">parabolic trough</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Compact_linear_Fresnel_reflector" title="Compact linear Fresnel reflector">compact linear Fresnel reflector</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy#Dish_designs" title="Solar thermal energy">Stirling dish</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_tower" title="Solar power tower">solar power tower</a>. Various techniques are used to track the sun and focus light. In all of these systems a <a href="/wiki/Working_fluid" title="Working fluid">working fluid</a> is heated by the concentrated sunlight, and is then used for power generation or energy storage.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin_2005_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_2005-10">[10]</a></sup> Thermal storage efficiently allows up to 24-hour electricity generation.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <i>parabolic trough</i> consists of a linear parabolic reflector that concentrates light onto a receiver positioned along the reflector's focal line. The receiver is a tube positioned right above the middle of the parabolic mirror and is filled with a working fluid. The reflector is made to follow the sun during daylight hours by tracking along a single axis. Parabolic trough systems provide the best land-use factor of any solar technology.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Solar_Energy_Generating_Systems" title="Solar Energy Generating Systems">SEGS</a> plants in California and Acciona's <a href="/wiki/Nevada_Solar_One" title="Nevada Solar One">Nevada Solar One</a> near <a href="/wiki/Boulder_City,_Nevada" title="Boulder City, Nevada">Boulder City, Nevada</a> are representatives of this technology.<sup id="cite_ref-SolarPaces_2001_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SolarPaces_2001-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Compact Linear Fresnel Reflectors</i> are CSP-plants which use many thin mirror strips instead of parabolic mirrors to concentrate sunlight onto two tubes with working fluid. This has the advantage that flat mirrors can be used which are much cheaper than parabolic mirrors, and that more reflectors can be placed in the same amount of space, allowing more of the available sunlight to be used. Concentrating linear fresnel reflectors can be used in either large or more compact plants.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Stirling solar dish</i> combines a parabolic concentrating dish with a <a href="/wiki/Stirling_engine" title="Stirling engine">Stirling engine</a> which normally drives an electric generator. The advantages of Stirling solar over photovoltaic cells are higher efficiency of converting sunlight into electricity and longer lifetime. Parabolic dish systems give the highest efficiency among CSP technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The 50&#160;kW <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Dish_(solar_thermal)" title="The Big Dish (solar thermal)">Big Dish</a> in <a href="/wiki/Canberra" title="Canberra">Canberra</a>, Australia is an example of this technology.<sup id="cite_ref-SolarPaces_2001_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SolarPaces_2001-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <i>solar power tower</i> uses an array of tracking reflectors (<a href="/wiki/Heliostat" title="Heliostat">heliostats</a>) to concentrate light on a central receiver atop a tower. Power towers are more cost effective, offer higher efficiency and better energy storage capability among CSP technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-SolarPaces_2001_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SolarPaces_2001-13">[13]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/PS10_Solar_Power_Plant" class="mw-redirect" title="PS10 Solar Power Plant">PS10 Solar Power Plant</a> and <a href="/wiki/PS20_solar_power_plant" title="PS20 solar power plant">PS20 solar power plant</a> are examples of this technology.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hybrid_systems">Hybrid systems</span></h4>
<dl>
<dd>A hybrid system combines (C)PV and CSP with one another or with other forms of generation such as diesel, wind and <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a>. The combined form of generation may enable the system to modulate power output as a function of demand or at least reduce the fluctuating nature of solar power and the consumption of non renewable fuel. Hybrid systems are most often found on islands.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dt>CPV/CSP system</dt>
<dd>A novel solar CPV/CSP hybrid system has been proposed, combining concentrator photovoltaics with the non-PV technology of concentrated solar power, or also known as concentrated solar thermal.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Combined_cycle#Integrated_solar_combined_cycle_.28ISCC.29" title="Combined cycle">ISCC</a> system</dt>
<dd>The <a href="/wiki/Hassi_R%27Mel_integrated_solar_combined_cycle_power_station" title="Hassi R'Mel integrated solar combined cycle power station">Hassi R'Mel power station</a> in Algeria, is an example of combining CSP with a gas turbine, where a 25-megawatt CSP-<a href="/wiki/Parabolic_trough" title="Parabolic trough">parabolic trough</a> array supplements a much larger 130&#160;MW <a href="/wiki/Combined_cycle" title="Combined cycle">combined cycle gas turbine plant</a>. Another example is the <a href="/wiki/Yazd_integrated_solar_combined_cycle_power_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Yazd integrated solar combined cycle power station">Yazd power station</a> in Iran.</dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_thermal_hybrid_solar_collector" title="Photovoltaic thermal hybrid solar collector">PVT system</a></dt>
<dd>Hybrid PV/T), also known as <i>photovoltaic thermal hybrid solar collectors</i> convert solar radiation into thermal and electrical energy. Such a system combines a solar (PV) module with a <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_collector" title="Solar thermal collector">solar thermal collector</a> in an complementary way.</dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Concentrated_photovoltaics_and_thermal" class="mw-redirect" title="Concentrated photovoltaics and thermal">CPVT system</a></dt>
<dd>A concentrated photovoltaic thermal hybrid (CPVT) system is similar to a PVT system. It uses <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Concentrated photovoltaic">concentrated photovoltaics</a> (CPV) instead of conventional PV technology, and combines it with a solar thermal collector.</dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/PV_diesel_system" class="mw-redirect" title="PV diesel system">PV diesel system</a></dt>
<dd>It combines a photovoltaic system with a <a href="/wiki/Diesel_generator" title="Diesel generator">diesel generator</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Combinations with <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_renewable_energy_system" title="Hybrid renewable energy system">other renewables</a> are possible and include <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbines" class="mw-redirect" title="Wind turbines">wind turbines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-20">[20]</a></sup></dd>
<dt>PV-<a href="/wiki/Thermogenerator" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermogenerator">thermoelectric</a> system</dt>
<dd>Thermoelectric, or "thermovoltaic" devices convert a temperature difference between dissimilar materials into an electric current. Solar cells use only the high frequency part of the radiation, while the low frequency heat energy is wasted. Several patents about the use of thermoelectric devices in tandem with solar cells have been filed.<sup id="cite_ref-Kraemer_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kraemer-21">[21]</a></sup> The idea is to increase the efficiency of the combined solar/thermoelectric system to convert the solar radiation into useful electricity.</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>

<td colspan="3" style="text-align: left; font-size: 0.85em; background-color: #f2f2f2;"><b>Source</b>: <a href="/wiki/BP" title="BP">BP</a>-Statistical Review of World Energy, 2016<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_days">Early days</span></h3>
<p>The early development of solar technologies starting in the 1860s was driven by an expectation that coal would soon become scarce. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Fritts" title="Charles Fritts">Charles Fritts</a> installed the world's first rooftop photovoltaic solar array, using 1%-efficient <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a> cells, on a New York City roof in 1884.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> However, development of solar technologies stagnated in the early 20th&#160;century in the face of the increasing availability, economy, and utility of coal and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In 1974 it was estimated that only six private homes in all of North America were entirely heated or cooled by functional solar power systems.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil embargo</a> and <a href="/wiki/1979_energy_crisis" title="1979 energy crisis">1979 energy crisis</a> caused a reorganization of energy policies around the world and brought renewed attention to developing solar technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Deployment strategies focused on incentive programs such as the Federal Photovoltaic Utilization Program in the US and the Sunshine Program in Japan. Other efforts included the formation of research facilities in the United States (SERI, now <a href="/wiki/NREL" class="mw-redirect" title="NREL">NREL</a>), Japan (<a href="/wiki/New_Energy_and_Industrial_Technology_Development_Organization" title="New Energy and Industrial Technology Development Organization">NEDO</a>), and Germany (<a href="/wiki/Fraunhofer_Society" title="Fraunhofer Society">Fraunhofer–ISE</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Between 1970 and 1983 installations of photovoltaic systems grew rapidly, but falling oil prices in the early 1980s moderated the <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_photovoltaics" title="Growth of photovoltaics">growth of photovoltaics</a> from 1984 to 1996.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-1990s_to_early_2010s">Mid-1990s to early 2010s</span></h3>
<p>In the mid-1990s, development of both, residential and commercial <a href="/wiki/Rooftop_solar" class="mw-redirect" title="Rooftop solar">rooftop solar</a> as well as utility-scale <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_power_station" title="Photovoltaic power station">photovoltaic power stations</a>, began to accelerate again due to supply issues with oil and natural gas, <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">global warming concerns</a>, and the improving economic position of PV relative to other energy technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In the early 2000s, the adoption of <a href="/wiki/Feed-in_tariff" title="Feed-in tariff">feed-in tariffs</a>—a policy mechanism, that gives renewables priority on the grid and defines a fixed price for the generated electricity—lead to a high level of investment security and to a soaring number of PV deployments in Europe.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_status">Current status</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_photovoltaics" title="Growth of photovoltaics">Growth of photovoltaics</a></div>
<p>For several years, worldwide growth of solar PV was driven by <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar power in the European Union">European deployment</a>, but has since shifted to Asia, especially <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_China" title="Solar power in China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Japan" title="Solar power in Japan">Japan</a>, and to a growing number of countries and regions all over the world, including, but not limited to, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Australia" title="Solar power in Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Canada" title="Solar power in Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Chile" title="Solar power in Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_India" title="Solar power in India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Israel" title="Solar power in Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Mexico" title="Solar power in Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_South_Africa" title="Solar power in South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_South_Korea" title="Energy in South Korea">South Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Thailand" title="Solar power in Thailand">Thailand</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_States" title="Solar power in the United States">United States</a>.</p>
<p>Worldwide growth of photovoltaics has averaged 40% per year since 2000 and total installed capacity reached 139&#160;GW at the end of 2013 with <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Germany" title="Solar power in Germany">Germany</a> having the most cumulative installations (35.7&#160;GW) and <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Italy" title="Solar power in Italy">Italy</a> having the highest percentage of electricity generated by solar PV (7.0%).<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The largest manufacturers are located in China.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Concentrated solar power (CSP) also started to grow rapidly, increasing its capacity nearly tenfold from 2004 to 2013, albeit from a lower level and involving fewer countries than solar PV.<sup id="cite_ref-ren21-gsr-2014_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ren21-gsr-2014-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:51</sup> As of the end of 2013, <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power#Deployment_around_the_world" title="Concentrated solar power">worldwide cumulative CSP-capacity</a> reached 3,425&#160;MW.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forecasts">Forecasts</span></h3>
<p>In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> predicted that global solar PV capacity could reach 3,000 GW or 11% of projected global electricity generation by 2050—enough to generate 4,500&#160;<a href="/wiki/Terawatt-hour" class="mw-redirect" title="Terawatt-hour">TWh</a> of electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Four years later, in 2014, the agency projected that, under its "high renewables" scenario, solar power could supply 27% of global electricity generation by 2050 (16% from PV and 11% from CSP).<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014-2">[2]</a></sup> In 2015, analysts predicted that one million homes in the U.S. will have solar power by the end of 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photovoltaic_power_stations">Photovoltaic power stations</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_photovoltaic_power_stations" title="List of photovoltaic power stations">List of photovoltaic power stations</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Desert_Sunlight_Solar_Farm" title="Desert Sunlight Solar Farm">Desert Sunlight Solar Farm</a> is a 550&#160;MW power plant in <a href="/wiki/Riverside_County,_California" title="Riverside County, California">Riverside County, California</a>, that uses <a href="/wiki/Thin_film_solar_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Thin film solar cell">thin-film</a> <a href="/wiki/Cadmium_telluride_photovoltaics" title="Cadmium telluride photovoltaics">CdTe-modules</a> made by <a href="/wiki/First_Solar" title="First Solar">First Solar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rew11_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rew11-38">[38]</a></sup> As of November 2014, the 550 megawatt <a href="/wiki/Topaz_Solar_Farm" title="Topaz Solar Farm">Topaz Solar Farm</a> was the largest photovoltaic power plant in the world. This was surpassed by the 579 MW <a href="/wiki/Solar_Star" title="Solar Star">Solar Star</a> complex. The current largest photovoltaic power station in the world is <a href="/wiki/Longyangxia_Dam" title="Longyangxia Dam">Longyangxia Dam</a> Solar Park, in <a href="/wiki/Gonghe_County" title="Gonghe County">Gonghe County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qinghai" title="Qinghai">Qinghai</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concentrating_solar_power_stations">Concentrating solar power stations</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_solar_thermal_power_stations" title="List of solar thermal power stations">List of solar thermal power stations</a></div>
<p>Commercial concentrating solar power (CSP) plants, also called "solar thermal power stations", were first developed in the 1980s. The 377&#160;MW <a href="/wiki/Ivanpah_Solar_Power_Facility" title="Ivanpah Solar Power Facility">Ivanpah Solar Power Facility</a>, located in California's Mojave Desert, is the world’s largest solar thermal power plant project. Other large CSP plants include the <a href="/wiki/Solnova_Solar_Power_Station" title="Solnova Solar Power Station">Solnova Solar Power Station</a> (150&#160;MW), the <a href="/wiki/Andasol_solar_power_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Andasol solar power station">Andasol solar power station</a> (150&#160;MW), and <a href="/wiki/Extresol_Solar_Power_Station" title="Extresol Solar Power Station">Extresol Solar Power Station</a> (150&#160;MW), all in Spain. The principal advantage of CSP is the ability to efficiently add thermal storage, allowing the dispatching of electricity over up to a 24-hour period. Since peak electricity demand typically occurs at about 5&#160;pm, many CSP power plants use 3 to 5&#160;hours of thermal storage.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cost">Cost</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:229px;max-width:229px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Swansons-law.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Swansons-law.svg/225px-Swansons-law.svg.png" width="225" height="169" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Swansons-law.svg/338px-Swansons-law.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Swansons-law.svg/450px-Swansons-law.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Swanson%27s_law" title="Swanson's law">Swanson's law</a> – the PV learning curve</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png/225px-EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png" width="225" height="161" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png/338px-EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png/450px-EU-PV-LCOE-Projection.png 2x" data-file-width="817" data-file-height="586" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Solar PV – <a href="/wiki/Levelized_cost_of_electricity" class="mw-redirect" title="Levelized cost of electricity">LCOE</a> for Europe until 2020 (in euro-cts. per <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilowatt-hour">kWh</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-EPIA2011Sep_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPIA2011Sep-46">[46]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Economic_Solar_PV_Capacity_vs_Installation_Cost.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/US_Economic_Solar_PV_Capacity_vs_Installation_Cost.png/225px-US_Economic_Solar_PV_Capacity_vs_Installation_Cost.png" width="225" height="169" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/US_Economic_Solar_PV_Capacity_vs_Installation_Cost.png/338px-US_Economic_Solar_PV_Capacity_vs_Installation_Cost.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/US_Economic_Solar_PV_Capacity_vs_Installation_Cost.png/450px-US_Economic_Solar_PV_Capacity_vs_Installation_Cost.png 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Economic photovoltaic capacity vs installation cost, in the United States</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adjusting for inflation, it cost $96 per watt for a solar module in the mid-1970s. Process improvements and a very large boost in production have brought that figure down to 68 cents per watt in February 2016, according to data from Bloomberg New Energy Finance.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Palo_Alto" class="mw-redirect" title="Palo Alto">Palo Alto</a> California signed a wholesale purchase agreement in 2016 that secured solar power for 3.7 cents per kilowatt-hour. And in sunny <a href="/wiki/Dubai" title="Dubai">Dubai</a> large-scale solar generated electricity sold in 2016 for just 2.99 cents per kilowatt-hour -- "competitive with any form of fossil-based electricity — and cheaper than most."<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Photovoltaic systems use no fuel, and modules typically last 25 to 40 years. Thus, capital costs make up most of the cost of solar power. Operations and maintenance costs for new utility-scale solar plants in the US are estimated to be 9 percent of the cost of photovoltaic electricity, and 17 percent of the cost of solar thermal electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Governments have created various financial incentives to encourage the use of solar power, such as <a href="/wiki/Feed-in_tariff" title="Feed-in tariff">feed-in tariff programs</a>. Also, <a href="/wiki/Renewable_portfolio_standard" title="Renewable portfolio standard">Renewable portfolio standards</a> impose a government mandate that utilities generate or acquire a certain percentage of renewable power regardless of increased energy procurement costs. In most states, RPS goals can be achieved by any combination of solar, wind, biomass, <a href="/wiki/Landfill_gas" title="Landfill gas">landfill gas</a>, ocean, geothermal, <a href="/wiki/Municipal_solid_waste" title="Municipal solid waste">municipal solid waste</a>, hydroelectric, hydrogen, or fuel cell technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-ajelp.com_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ajelp.com-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Levelized_cost_of_electricity">Levelized cost of electricity</span></h4>
<p>The PV industry is beginning to adopt <a href="/wiki/Levelized_cost_of_electricity" class="mw-redirect" title="Levelized cost of electricity">levelized cost of electricity</a> (LCOE) as the unit of cost. The electrical energy generated is sold in units of <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilowatt-hour">kilowatt-hours</a> (kWh). As a rule of thumb, and depending on the local <a href="/wiki/Insolation" class="mw-redirect" title="Insolation">insolation</a>, 1 watt-peak of installed solar PV capacity generates about 1 to 2&#160;kWh of electricity per year. This corresponds to a <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factor</a> of around 10–20%. The product of the local cost of electricity and the insolation determines the break even point for solar power. The International Conference on Solar Photovoltaic Investments, organized by <a href="/wiki/European_Photovoltaic_Industry_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="European Photovoltaic Industry Association">EPIA</a>, has estimated that PV systems will pay back their investors in 8 to 12 years.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> As a result, since 2006 it has been economical for investors to install photovoltaics for free in return for a long term <a href="/wiki/Power_purchase_agreement" title="Power purchase agreement">power purchase agreement</a>. Fifty percent of commercial systems in the United States were installed in this manner in 2007 and over 90% by 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Shi_Zhengrong" title="Shi Zhengrong">Shi Zhengrong</a> has said that, as of 2012, unsubsidised solar power is already competitive with fossil fuels in India, Hawaii, Italy and Spain. He said "We are at a tipping point. No longer are renewable power sources like solar and wind a luxury of the rich. They are now starting to compete in the real world without subsidies". "Solar power will be able to compete without subsidies against conventional power sources in half the world by 2015".<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_installation_prices">Current installation prices</span></h4>
<p>In its 2014 edition of the <i>Technology Roadmap: Solar Photovoltaic Energy</i> report, the International Energy Agency (IEA) published prices for residential, commercial and utility-scale <a href="/wiki/PV_system" class="mw-redirect" title="PV system">PV systems</a> for eight major markets as of 2013 <i>(see table below)</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014-2">[2]</a></sup> However, DOE's <a href="/wiki/SVTC_Technologies#The_SunShot_Initiative" title="SVTC Technologies">SunShot Initiative</a> has reported much lower U.S. installation prices. In 2014, prices continued to decline. The SunShot Initiative modeled U.S. system prices to be in the range of $1.80 to $3.29 per watt.<sup id="cite_ref-sunshot-2014_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sunshot-2014-54">[54]</a></sup> Other sources identify similar price ranges of $1.70 to $3.50 for the different market segments in the U.S.,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> and in the highly penetrated German market, prices for residential and small commercial rooftop systems of up to 100&#160;kW declined to $1.36 per watt (€1.24/W) by the end of 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In 2015, Deutsche Bank estimated costs for small residential rooftop systems in the U.S. around $2.90 per watt. Costs for utility-scale systems in China and India were estimated as low as $1.00 per watt.<sup id="cite_ref-deutsche-2015-chasm_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deutsche-2015-chasm-57">[57]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grid_parity">Grid parity</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Grid_parity" title="Grid parity">Grid parity</a></div>
<p>Grid parity, the point at which the cost of photovoltaic electricity is equal to or cheaper than the price of <a href="/wiki/Mains_electricity" title="Mains electricity">grid power</a>, is more easily achieved in areas with abundant sun and high costs for electricity such as in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> In 2008, The levelized cost of electricity for solar PV was $0.25/kWh or less in most of the <a href="/wiki/OECD" class="mw-redirect" title="OECD">OECD</a> countries. By late 2011, the fully loaded cost was predicted to fall below $0.15/kWh for most of the <a href="/wiki/OECD" class="mw-redirect" title="OECD">OECD</a> and to reach $0.10/kWh in sunnier regions. These cost levels are driving three emerging trends: vertical integration of the supply chain, origination of <a href="/wiki/Power_purchase_agreement" title="Power purchase agreement">power purchase agreements</a> (PPAs) by solar power companies, and unexpected risk for traditional power generation companies, <a href="/wiki/Grid_operator" class="mw-redirect" title="Grid operator">grid operators</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine_manufacturer" class="mw-redirect" title="Wind turbine manufacturer">wind turbine manufacturers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-truecost_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-truecost-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title="&#160;Dead link since June 2015">dead link</span></a></i>]</span></sup></p>
<p>Grid parity was first reached in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> in 2013,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> and other islands that otherwise use <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> (<a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel fuel</a>) to produce electricity, and most of the US is expected to reach grid parity by 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (June 2015)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2007, <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a>'s Chief Engineer predicted grid parity without subsidies in sunny parts of the United States by around 2015; other companies predicted an earlier date:<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> the cost of solar power will be below grid parity for more than half of residential customers and 10% of commercial customers in the <a href="/wiki/OECD" class="mw-redirect" title="OECD">OECD</a>, as long as grid electricity prices do not decrease through 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-truecost_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-truecost-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Productivity_by_location">Productivity by location</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Solar_irradiance" title="Solar irradiance">Solar irradiance</a></div>
<p>Different parts of the world experience different amounts of sunshine, depending on <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> and weather. Locations nearer the equator receive many more hours of sunshine than those further north or south, thus photovoltaic panels produce more kWhs per year, and so can be far more economically desirable in some places more than others.</p>
