<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self_consumption">Self consumption</span></h3>
<p>In cases of self consumption of the solar energy, the payback time is calculated based on how much electricity is not purchased from the grid. For example, in Germany, with electricity prices of 0.25 Euro/KWh and <a href="/wiki/Insolation" class="mw-redirect" title="Insolation">insolation</a> of 900 KWh/KW, one KWp will save 225 Euro per year, and with an installation cost of 1700 Euro/KWp the system cost will be returned in less than 7 years.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> However, in many cases, the patterns of generation and consumption do not coincide, and some or all of the energy is fed back into the grid. The electricity is sold, and at other times when energy is taken from the grid, electricity is bought. The relative costs and prices obtained affect the economics. In many markets, the price paid for sold PV electricity is significantly lower than the price of bought electricity, which incentivizes self consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2014_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2014-65">[65]</a></sup> Moreover, separate self consumption incentives have been used in e.g. Germany and Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2014_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2014-65">[65]</a></sup> Grid interaction regulation has also included limitations of grid feed-in in some regions in Germany with high amounts of installed PV capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-trends-2014_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-trends-2014-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> By increasing self consumption, the grid feed-in can be limited without curtailment, which wastes electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-salpakari-lund-2016_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salpakari-lund-2016-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>A good match between generation and consumption is key for high self consumption, and should be considered when deciding where to install solar power and how to dimension the installation. The match can be improved with batteries or controllable electricity consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-salpakari-lund-2016_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salpakari-lund-2016-67">[67]</a></sup> However, batteries are expensive and profitability may require provision of other services from them besides self consumption increase.<sup id="cite_ref-rmi-battery-2015_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rmi-battery-2015-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hot_water_storage_tank" title="Hot water storage tank">Hot water storage tanks</a> with electric heating with heat pumps or resistance heaters can provide low-cost storage for self consumption of solar power.<sup id="cite_ref-salpakari-lund-2016_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salpakari-lund-2016-67">[67]</a></sup> Shiftable loads, such as dishwashers, tumble dryers and washing machines, can provide controllable consumption with only a limited effect on the users, but their effect on self consumption of solar power may be limited.<sup id="cite_ref-salpakari-lund-2016_67-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salpakari-lund-2016-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_pricing_and_incentives">Energy pricing and incentives</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/PV_financial_incentives" class="mw-redirect" title="PV financial incentives">PV financial incentives</a></div>
<p>The political purpose of incentive policies for PV is to facilitate an initial small-scale deployment to begin to grow the industry, even where the cost of PV is significantly above grid parity, to allow the industry to achieve the economies of scale necessary to reach grid parity. The policies are implemented to promote national energy independence, high tech job creation and reduction of CO<sub>2</sub> emissions. Three incentive mechanisms are often used in combination as investment subsidies: the authorities refund part of the cost of installation of the system, the electricity utility buys PV electricity from the producer under a multiyear contract at a guaranteed rate, and <a href="/wiki/Solar_Renewable_Energy_Certificates" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar Renewable Energy Certificates">Solar Renewable Energy Certificates</a> (SRECs)</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rebates">Rebates</span></h4>
<p>With investment subsidies, the financial burden falls upon the taxpayer, while with feed-in tariffs the extra cost is distributed across the utilities' customer bases. While the investment subsidy may be simpler to administer, the main argument in favour of feed-in tariffs is the encouragement of quality. Investment subsidies are paid out as a function of the nameplate capacity of the installed system and are independent of its actual power yield over time, thus rewarding the overstatement of power and tolerating poor durability and maintenance. Some electric companies offer rebates to their customers, such as <a href="/wiki/Austin_Energy" title="Austin Energy">Austin Energy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, which offers $2.50/watt installed up to $15,000.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Net_metering">Net metering</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Feed-in_Tariff_meter_connections.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Feed-in_Tariff_meter_connections.png/250px-Feed-in_Tariff_meter_connections.png" width="250" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Feed-in_Tariff_meter_connections.png/375px-Feed-in_Tariff_meter_connections.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Feed-in_Tariff_meter_connections.png/500px-Feed-in_Tariff_meter_connections.png 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="900" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Feed-in_Tariff_meter_connections.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Net_metering" title="Net metering">Net metering</a>, unlike a <a href="/wiki/Feed-in_tariff" title="Feed-in tariff">feed-in tariff</a>, requires only one meter, but it must be bi-directional.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Net_metering" title="Net metering">net metering</a> the price of the electricity produced is the same as the price supplied to the consumer, and the consumer is billed on the difference between production and consumption. Net metering can usually be done with no changes to standard <a href="/wiki/Electricity_meter" title="Electricity meter">electricity meters</a>, which accurately measure power in both directions and automatically report the difference, and because it allows homeowners and businesses to generate electricity at a different time from consumption, effectively using the grid as a giant storage battery. With net metering, deficits are billed each month while surpluses are rolled over to the following month. Best practices call for perpetual roll over of kWh credits.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Excess credits upon termination of service are either lost, or paid for at a rate ranging from wholesale to retail rate or above, as can be excess annual credits. In New Jersey, annual excess credits are paid at the wholesale rate, as are left over credits when a customer terminates service.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Feed-in_tariffs_.28FIT.29">Feed-in tariffs (FIT)</span></h4>
<p>With <a href="/wiki/Feed-in_tariff" title="Feed-in tariff">feed-in tariffs</a>, the financial burden falls upon the consumer. They reward the number of kilowatt-hours produced over a long period of time, but because the rate is set by the authorities, it may result in perceived overpayment. The price paid per kilowatt-hour under a feed-in tariff exceeds the price of grid electricity. Net metering refers to the case where the price paid by the utility is the same as the price charged.</p>
<p>The complexity of approvals in California, Spain and Italy has prevented comparable growth to Germany even though the return on investment is better.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In some countries, additional incentives are offered for <a href="/wiki/Building-integrated_photovoltaics" title="Building-integrated photovoltaics">BIPV</a> compared to stand alone PV.</p>
<ul>
<li>France + EUR 0.16 /kWh (compared to semi-integrated) or + EUR 0.27/kWh (compared to stand alone)</li>
<li>Italy + EUR 0.04-0.09 kWh</li>
<li>Germany + EUR 0.05/kWh (facades only)</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_Renewable_Energy_Credits_.28SRECs.29">Solar Renewable Energy Credits (SRECs)</span></h4>
<p>Alternatively, <a href="/wiki/Solar_Renewable_Energy_Certificate" title="Solar Renewable Energy Certificate">SRECs</a> allow for a market mechanism to set the price of the solar generated electricity subsity. In this mechanism, a renewable energy production or consumption target is set, and the utility (more technically the Load Serving Entity) is obliged to purchase renewable energy or face a fine (Alternative Compliance Payment or ACP). The producer is credited for an SREC for every 1,000&#160;kWh of electricity produced. If the utility buys this SREC and retires it, they avoid paying the ACP. In principle this system delivers the cheapest renewable energy, since the all solar facilities are eligible and can be installed in the most economic locations. Uncertainties about the future value of SRECs have led to long-term SREC contract markets to give clarity to their prices and allow solar developers to pre-sell and hedge their credits.</p>
<p>Financial incentives for photovoltaics differ across countries, including <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Australia" title="Solar power in Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_China" title="Solar power in China">China</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Germany" title="Solar power in Germany">Germany</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-German_PV_market_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-German_PV_market-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Israel" title="Solar power in Israel">Israel</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Japan" title="Solar power in Japan">Japan</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_States" title="Solar power in the United States">United States</a> and even across states within the US.</p>
<p>The Japanese government through its <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_International_Trade_and_Industry" title="Ministry of International Trade and Industry">Ministry of International Trade and Industry</a> ran a successful programme of subsidies from 1994 to 2003. By the end of 2004, Japan led the world in installed PV capacity with over 1.1&#160;<a href="/wiki/Gigawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigawatt">GW</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2004, the German government introduced the first large-scale feed-in tariff system, under the <a href="/wiki/German_Renewable_Energy_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="German Renewable Energy Act">German Renewable Energy Act</a>, which resulted in explosive growth of PV installations in Germany. At the outset the FIT was over 3x the retail price or 8x the industrial price. The principle behind the German system is a 20-year flat rate contract. The value of new contracts is programmed to decrease each year, in order to encourage the industry to pass on lower costs to the end users. The programme has been more successful than expected with over 1GW installed in 2006, and political pressure is mounting to decrease the tariff to lessen the future burden on consumers.</p>
<p>Subsequently, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Spain" title="Solar power in Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Italy" title="Solar power in Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Greece" title="Solar power in Greece">Greece</a>—that enjoyed an early success with domestic solar-thermal installations for hot water needs—and <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_France" title="Solar power in France">France</a> introduced feed-in tariffs. None have replicated the programmed decrease of FIT in new contracts though, making the German incentive relatively less and less attractive compared to other countries. The French and Greek FIT offer a high premium (EUR 0.55/kWh) for building integrated systems. California, Greece, France and Italy have 30-50% more insolation than Germany making them financially more attractive. The Greek domestic "solar roof" programme (adopted in June 2009 for installations up to 10&#160;kW) has internal rates of return of 10-15% at current commercial installation costs, which, furthermore, is tax free.</p>
<p>In 2006 <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_California" title="Solar power in California">California</a> approved the '<a href="/wiki/California_Solar_Initiative" class="mw-redirect" title="California Solar Initiative">California Solar Initiative</a>', offering a choice of investment subsidies or FIT for small and medium systems and a FIT for large systems. The small-system FIT of $0.39 per kWh (far less than EU countries) expires in just 5 years, and the alternate "EPBB" residential investment incentive is modest, averaging perhaps 20% of cost. All California incentives are scheduled to decrease in the future depending as a function of the amount of PV capacity installed.</p>
<p>At the end of 2006, the Ontario Power Authority (OPA, Canada) began its Standard Offer Program, a precursor to the <a href="/wiki/Green_Energy_Act_2009" title="Green Energy Act 2009">Green Energy Act</a>, and the first in North America for distributed renewable projects of less than 10&#160;MW. The feed-in tariff guaranteed a fixed price of $0.42 CDN per kWh over a period of twenty years. Unlike net metering, all the electricity produced was sold to the OPA at the given rate.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impacts">Environmental impacts</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Blick_vom_aussichtsturm_h%C3%B6rlitz4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Blick_vom_aussichtsturm_h%C3%B6rlitz4.jpg/220px-Blick_vom_aussichtsturm_h%C3%B6rlitz4.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Blick_vom_aussichtsturm_h%C3%B6rlitz4.jpg/330px-Blick_vom_aussichtsturm_h%C3%B6rlitz4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Blick_vom_aussichtsturm_h%C3%B6rlitz4.jpg/440px-Blick_vom_aussichtsturm_h%C3%B6rlitz4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1673" data-file-height="1056" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Blick_vom_aussichtsturm_h%C3%B6rlitz4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Part of the <a href="/wiki/Senftenberg_Solarpark" title="Senftenberg Solarpark">Senftenberg Solarpark</a>, a solar <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Photovoltaic">photovoltaic</a> power plant located on former open-pit mining areas close to the city of <a href="/wiki/Senftenberg" title="Senftenberg">Senftenberg</a>, in Eastern Germany. The 78 MW Phase 1 of the plant was completed within three months.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Unlike <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> based technologies, solar power does not lead to any harmful emissions during operation, but the production of the panels leads to some amount of pollution.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenhouse_gases">Greenhouse gases</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_greenhouse-gas_emissions_of_energy_sources" title="Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources">Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of solar power</a> are in the range of 22 to 46&#160;gram (g) per <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilowatt-hour">kilowatt-hour</a> (kWh) depending on if solar thermal or solar PV is being analyzed, respectively. With this potentially being decreased to 15&#160;g/kWh in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-ECN2006_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ECN2006-76">[76]</a></sup> For comparison (of weighted averages), a <a href="/wiki/Combined_cycle" title="Combined cycle">combined cycle</a> gas-fired power plant emits some 400–599&#160;g/kWh,<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-77">[77]</a></sup> an oil-fired power plant 893&#160;g/kWh,<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-77">[77]</a></sup> a coal-fired power plant 915–994&#160;g/kWh<sup id="cite_ref-utilization_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utilization-78">[78]</a></sup> or with <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a> some 200&#160;g/kWh, and a <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">geothermal</a> high-temp. power plant 91–122&#160;g/kWh.<sup id="cite_ref-IPCC_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-77">[77]</a></sup> The life cycle emission intensity of <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> are lower than solar's as of 2011 as published by the IPCC, and discussed in the article <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_greenhouse-gas_emissions_of_energy_sources" title="Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources">Life-cycle greenhouse-gas emissions of energy sources</a>. Similar to all energy sources were their total life cycle emissions primarily lay in the construction and transportation phase, the switch to <a href="/wiki/Low_carbon_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Low carbon power">low carbon power</a> in the manufacturing and transportation of solar devices would further reduce carbon emissions. BP Solar owns two factories built by Solarex (one in Maryland, the other in Virginia) in which all of the energy used to manufacture solar panels is produced by solar panels. A 1-kilowatt system eliminates the burning of approximately 170 pounds of coal, 300 pounds of carbon dioxide from being released into the atmosphere, and saves up to 105 gallons of water consumption monthly.<sup id="cite_ref-Solar_Energy_Facts_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Solar_Energy_Facts-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>The US National Renewable Energy Laboratory (<a href="/wiki/NREL" class="mw-redirect" title="NREL">NREL</a>), in harmonizing the disparate estimates of life-cycle GHG emissions for solar PV, found that the most critical parameter was the solar insolation of the site: GHG emissions factors for PV solar are inversely proportional to insolation.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> For a site with insolation of 1700 kWh/m2/year, typical of southern Europe, NREL researchers estimated GHG emissions of 45&#160;gCO<sub>2</sub>e/kWh. Using the same assumptions, at Phoenix, USA, with insolation of 2400&#160;kWh/m2/year, the GHG emissions factor would be reduced to 32&#160;g of CO<sub>2</sub>e/kWh.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Commissioner_for_the_Environment" title="Parliamentary Commissioner for the Environment">Parliamentary Commissioner for the Environment</a> found that the solar PV would have little impact on the country's greenhouse gas emissions. The country already generates 80 percent of its electricity from renewable resources (primarily hydroelectricity and geothermal) and national electricity usage peaks on winter evenings whereas solar generation peaks on summer afternoons, meaning a large uptake of solar PV would end up displacing other renewable generators before fossil-fueled power plants.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_payback">Energy payback</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon#Energy_payback_time" title="Crystalline silicon">energy payback time</a> (EPBT) of a power generating system is the time required to generate as much energy as is consumed during production and lifetime operation of the system. Due to improving production technologies the payback time has been decreasing constantly since the introduction of PV systems in the energy market.<sup id="cite_ref-Fraunhofer-PR-2014_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fraunhofer-PR-2014-83">[83]</a></sup> In 2000 the energy payback time of PV systems was estimated as 8 to 11 years<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> and in 2006 this was estimated to be 1.5 to 3.5 years for <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon" title="Crystalline silicon">crystalline silicon</a> silicon PV systems<sup id="cite_ref-ECN2006_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ECN2006-76">[76]</a></sup> and 1–1.5 years for thin film technologies (S. Europe).<sup id="cite_ref-ECN2006_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ECN2006-76">[76]</a></sup> These figures fell to 0.75–3.5 years in 2013, with an average of about 2 years for crystalline silicon PV and CIS systems.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another economic measure, closely related to the energy payback time, is the <a href="/wiki/EROEI" class="mw-redirect" title="EROEI">energy returned on energy invested</a> (EROEI) or <a href="/wiki/EROEI" class="mw-redirect" title="EROEI">energy return on investment</a> (EROI),<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> which is the ratio of electricity generated divided by the energy required to build <i>and maintain</i> the equipment. (This is not the same as the <a href="/wiki/Return_on_investment" title="Return on investment">economic return on investment</a> (ROI), which varies according to local energy prices, subsidies available and metering techniques.) With expected lifetimes of 30 years,<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> the EROEI of PV systems are in the range of 10 to 30, thus generating enough energy over their lifetimes to reproduce themselves many times (6-31 reproductions) depending on what type of material, <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_system" title="Balance of system">balance of system</a> (BOS), and the geographic location of the system.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_issues">Other issues</span></h3>
<p>One issue that has often raised concerns is the use of <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a> (Cd), a <a href="/wiki/Toxic_heavy_metal" title="Toxic heavy metal">toxic heavy metal</a> that has the tendency to <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">accumulate</a> in ecological <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chains</a>. It is used as semiconductor component in <a href="/wiki/Cadmium_telluride_solar_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Cadmium telluride solar cell">CdTe solar cells</a> and as buffer layer for certain <a href="/wiki/CIGS_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="CIGS cell">CIGS cells</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Cadmium_sulfide" title="Cadmium sulfide">CdS</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-werner_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-werner-89">[89]</a></sup> The amount of cadmium used in <a href="/wiki/Thin-film_solar_cell" title="Thin-film solar cell">thin-film PV modules</a> is relatively small (5–10 g/m²) and with proper recycling and emission control techniques in place the cadmium emissions from module production can be almost zero. Current PV technologies lead to cadmium emissions of 0.3–0.9 <a href="/wiki/Microgram" title="Microgram">microgram</a>/kWh over the whole life-cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-ECN2006_76-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ECN2006-76">[76]</a></sup> Most of these emissions actually arise through the use of coal power for the manufacturing of the modules, and coal and <a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">lignite</a> combustion leads to much higher emissions of cadmium. Life-cycle cadmium emissions from coal is 3.1 microgram/kWh, lignite 6.2, and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> 0.2 microgram/kWh.</p>
<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_analysis" class="mw-redirect" title="Life-cycle analysis">life-cycle analysis</a> it has been noted, that if electricity produced by photovoltaic panels were used to manufacture the modules instead of electricity from burning coal, cadmium emissions from coal power usage in the manufacturing process could be entirely eliminated.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the case of <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon" title="Crystalline silicon">crystalline silicon</a> modules, the <a href="/wiki/Solder" title="Solder">solder</a> material, that joins together the copper strings of the cells, contains about 36 percent of <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> (Pb). Moreover, the paste used for screen printing front and back contacts contains traces of Pb and sometimes Cd as well. It is estimated that about 1,000 metric tonnes of Pb have been used for 100 gigawatts of c-Si solar modules. However, there is no fundamental need for lead in the solder alloy.<sup id="cite_ref-werner_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-werner-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some media sources have reported that concentrated solar power plants have injured or killed large numbers of birds due to intense heat from the concentrated sunrays.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> This adverse effect does not apply to PV solar power plants, and some of the claims may have been overstated or exaggerated.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2014-published life-cycle analysis of land use for various sources of electricity concluded that the large-scale implementation of solar and wind potentially reduces pollution-related environmental impacts. The study found that the land-use footprint, given in square meter-years per megawatt-hour (m<sup>2</sup>a/MWh), was lowest for wind, natural gas and rooftop PV, with 0.26, 0.49 and 0.59, respectively, and followed by utility-scale solar PV with 7.9. For CSP, the footprint was 9 and 14, using parabolic troughs and solar towers, respectively. The largest footprint had coal-fired power plants with 18&#160;m<sup>2</sup>a/MWh.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emerging_technologies">Emerging technologies</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concentrator_photovoltaics">Concentrator photovoltaics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:3_MW_CPV_project_in_Golmud,_China.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/3_MW_CPV_project_in_Golmud%2C_China.jpg/220px-3_MW_CPV_project_in_Golmud%2C_China.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/3_MW_CPV_project_in_Golmud%2C_China.jpg/330px-3_MW_CPV_project_in_Golmud%2C_China.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/3_MW_CPV_project_in_Golmud%2C_China.jpg/440px-3_MW_CPV_project_in_Golmud%2C_China.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1154" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:3_MW_CPV_project_in_Golmud,_China.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Concentrator_photovoltaics" title="Concentrator photovoltaics">CPV modules</a> on dual axis <a href="/wiki/Solar_tracker" title="Solar tracker">solar trackers</a> in Golmud, China</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Concentrator_photovoltaics" title="Concentrator photovoltaics">Concentrator photovoltaics</a> (CPV) systems employ sunlight concentrated onto photovoltaic surfaces for the purpose of <a href="/wiki/Electrical_power_production" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical power production">electrical power production</a>. Contrary to conventional photovoltaic systems, it uses <a href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">lenses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Curved_mirror" title="Curved mirror">curved mirrors</a> to focus sunlight onto small, but highly efficient, <a href="/wiki/Multi-junction_solar_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Multi-junction solar cells">multi-junction solar cells</a>. Solar concentrators of all varieties may be used, and these are often mounted on a <a href="/wiki/Solar_tracker" title="Solar tracker">solar tracker</a> in order to keep the focal point upon the cell as the sun moves across the sky.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Luminescent_solar_concentrator" title="Luminescent solar concentrator">Luminescent solar concentrators</a> (when combined with a PV-solar cell) can also be regarded as a CPV system. Concentrated photovoltaics are useful as they can improve efficiency of PV-solar panels drastically.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>In addition, most <a href="/wiki/Solar_panels_on_spacecraft" title="Solar panels on spacecraft">solar panels on spacecraft</a> are also made of high efficient multi-junction photovoltaic cells to derive electricity from sunlight when operating in the <a href="/wiki/Inner_Solar_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Inner Solar System">inner Solar System</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Floatovoltaics">Floatovoltaics</span></h3>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Floatovoltaics" class="mw-redirect" title="Floatovoltaics">Floatovoltaics</a> are an emerging form of PV systems that float on the surface of irrigation canals, water reservoirs, quarry lakes, and tailing ponds. Several systems exist in France, India, Japan, Korea, the United Kingdom and the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> These systems reduce the need of valuable land area, save drinking water that would otherwise be lost through evaporation, and show a higher efficiency of solar <a href="/wiki/Energy_conversion" class="mw-redirect" title="Energy conversion">energy conversion</a>, as the panels are kept at a cooler temperature than they would be on land.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Although not floating, other dual-use facilities with solar power include <a href="/wiki/Fishery" title="Fishery">fisheries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grid_integration">Grid integration</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Energy_storage" title="Energy storage">Energy storage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grid_energy_storage" title="Grid energy storage">Grid energy storage</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abengoa_Solar_(7336087392).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Abengoa_Solar_%287336087392%29.jpg/220px-Abengoa_Solar_%287336087392%29.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Abengoa_Solar_%287336087392%29.jpg/330px-Abengoa_Solar_%287336087392%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Abengoa_Solar_%287336087392%29.jpg/440px-Abengoa_Solar_%287336087392%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3926" data-file-height="2495" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abengoa_Solar_(7336087392).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Construction of the Salt Tanks which provide efficient <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy_storage" title="Thermal energy storage">thermal energy storage</a><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> so that output can be provided after the sun goes down, and output can be scheduled to meet demand requirements.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The 280 MW <a href="/wiki/Solana_Generating_Station" title="Solana Generating Station">Solana Generating Station</a> is designed to provide six hours of energy storage. This allows the plant to generate about 38 percent of its rated capacity over the course of a year.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:229px;max-width:229px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:12-05-08_AS1.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/12-05-08_AS1.JPG/225px-12-05-08_AS1.JPG" width="225" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/12-05-08_AS1.JPG/338px-12-05-08_AS1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/12-05-08_AS1.JPG/450px-12-05-08_AS1.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3504" data-file-height="2336" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy_storage" title="Thermal energy storage">Thermal energy storage</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Andasol_solar_power_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Andasol solar power station">Andasol</a> CSP plant uses tanks of molten salt to store solar energy.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Geesthacht_Energiepark.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Geesthacht_Energiepark.jpg/225px-Geesthacht_Energiepark.jpg" width="225" height="136" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Geesthacht_Energiepark.jpg/338px-Geesthacht_Energiepark.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Geesthacht_Energiepark.jpg/450px-Geesthacht_Energiepark.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3537" data-file-height="2133" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">Pumped-storage hydroelectricity</a> (PSH). This facility in <a href="/wiki/Geesthacht" title="Geesthacht">Geesthacht</a>, Germany, also includes a solar array.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since solar energy is not available at night, <a href="/wiki/Energy_storage" title="Energy storage">storing its energy</a> is an important issue in order to have continuous energy availability.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Both <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a> and solar power are <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">variable renewable energy</a>, meaning that all available output must be taken when it is available, and either stored for <i>when it can be used later</i>, or transported over <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission" title="Electric power transmission">transmission</a> lines to <i>where it can be used now</i>. <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">Concentrated solar power</a> plants may use <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy_storage" title="Thermal energy storage">thermal energy storage</a> to store the solar energy, such as in high-temperature molten salts. These salts are an effective storage medium because they are low-cost, have a high specific heat capacity, and can deliver heat at temperatures compatible with conventional power systems. This method of energy storage is used, for example, by the <a href="/wiki/The_Solar_Project" title="The Solar Project">Solar Two</a> power station, allowing it to store 1.44&#160;<a href="/wiki/Terajoules" class="mw-redirect" title="Terajoules">TJ</a> in its 68&#160;m³ storage tank, enough to provide full output for close to 39 hours, with an efficiency of about 99%.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_batteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Rechargeable batteries">Rechargeable batteries</a> have been traditionally used to store excess electricity in <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_system#Standalone_system" title="Photovoltaic system">stand alone PV systems</a>. With <a href="/wiki/Grid-connected_photovoltaic_power_system" title="Grid-connected photovoltaic power system">grid-connected photovoltaic power system</a>, excess electricity can be sent to the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical grid</a>. <a href="/wiki/Net_metering" title="Net metering">Net metering</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feed-in_tariff" title="Feed-in tariff">feed-in tariff</a> programs give these systems a credit for the electricity they produce. This credit offsets electricity provided from the grid when the system cannot meet demand, effectively trading with the grid instead of storing excess electricity. Credits are normally rolled over from month to month and any remaining surplus settled annually.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> When wind and solar are a small fraction of the grid power, other generation techniques can adjust their output appropriately, but as these forms of variable power grow, additional balance on the grid is needed. As prices are rapidly declining, PV systems increasingly use rechargeable batteries to store a surplus to be later used at night. <a href="/wiki/Grid_energy_storage#Batteries" title="Grid energy storage">Batteries used for grid-storage</a> stabilize the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical grid</a> by <a href="/wiki/Grid_energy_storage#Load_leveling" title="Grid energy storage">leveling out peak loads</a> usually for several minutes, and in rare cases for hours. In the future, less expensive batteries could play an important role on the electrical grid, as they can charge during periods when generation exceeds demand and feed their stored energy into the grid when demand is higher than generation.</p>
<p>Although not permitted under the US National Electric Code, it is technically possible to have a “<a href="/wiki/Plug_and_play" title="Plug and play">plug and play</a>” PV microinverter. A recent review article found that careful system design would enable such systems to meet all technical, though not all safety requirements.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> There are several companies selling plug and play solar systems available on the web, but there is a concern that if people install their own it will reduce the enormous labor advantage solar has over <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>Common battery technologies used in today's home PV systems include, the <a href="/wiki/Valve_regulated_lead-acid_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Valve regulated lead-acid battery">valve regulated lead-acid battery</a>– a modified version of the conventional <a href="/wiki/Lead%E2%80%93acid_battery" title="Lead–acid battery">lead–acid battery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickel%E2%80%93cadmium_battery" title="Nickel–cadmium battery">nickel–cadmium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">lithium-ion</a> batteries. Lead-acid batteries are currently the predominant technology used in small-scale, residential PV systems, due to their high reliability, low self discharge and investment and maintenance costs, despite shorter lifetime and lower energy density. However, lithium-ion batteries have the potential to replace lead-acid batteries in the near future, as they are being intensively developed and lower prices are expected due to <a href="/wiki/Economies_of_scale" title="Economies of scale">economies of scale</a> provided by large production facilities such as the <a href="/wiki/Gigafactory_1" title="Gigafactory 1">Gigafactory 1</a>. In addition, the Li-ion batteries of plug-in <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">electric cars</a> may serve as a future storage devices in a <a href="/wiki/Vehicle-to-grid" title="Vehicle-to-grid">vehicle-to-grid</a> system. Since most vehicles are parked an average of 95 percent of the time, their batteries could be used to let electricity flow from the car to the power lines and back. Other rechargeable batteries used for <a href="/wiki/Distributed_energy_storage_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed energy storage system">distributed</a> PV systems include, <a href="/wiki/Sodium%E2%80%93sulfur_battery" title="Sodium–sulfur battery">sodium–sulfur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vanadium_redox_battery" title="Vanadium redox battery">vanadium redox</a> batteries, two prominent types of a <a href="/wiki/Molten_salt_battery" title="Molten salt battery">molten salt</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Flow_battery" title="Flow battery">flow</a> battery, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-ethz-harvard_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethz-harvard-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Conventional hydroelectricity works very well in conjunction with variable electricity sources such as solar and wind, the water can be held back and allowed to flow as required. Where a suitable river is not available, <a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">pumped-storage hydroelectricity</a> stores energy in the form of water pumped when surplus electricity is available, from a lower elevation reservoir to a higher elevation one. The energy is recovered when demand is high by releasing the water through a hydroelectric power generator.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> However, this cycle can lose 20% of the energy to round trip inefficiencies.</p>
<p>The combination of wind and solar PV has the advantage that the two sources complement each other because the peak operating times for each system occur at different times of the day and year. The power generation of such <a href="/wiki/Solar_hybrid_power_systems" title="Solar hybrid power systems">solar hybrid power systems</a> is therefore more constant and fluctuates less than each of the two component subsystems.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-20">[20]</a></sup> Solar power is seasonal, particularly in northern/southern climates, away from the equator, suggesting a need for long term seasonal storage in a medium such as hydrogen or pumped hydroelectric.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The Institute for Solar Energy Supply Technology of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Kassel" title="University of Kassel">University of Kassel</a> pilot-tested a <a href="/wiki/Virtual_power_plant" title="Virtual power plant">combined power plant</a> linking solar, wind, <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">hydrostorage</a> to provide load-following power from renewable sources.<sup id="cite_ref-combined_power_plant_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-combined_power_plant-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Research is also undertaken in this field of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_photosynthesis" title="Artificial photosynthesis">artificial photosynthesis</a>. It involves the use of <a href="/wiki/Nanotechnology" title="Nanotechnology">nanotechnology</a> to store solar electromagnetic energy in chemical bonds, by splitting water to produce <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> fuel or then combining with carbon dioxide to make biopolymers such as <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>. Many large national and regional research projects on artificial photosynthesis are now trying to develop techniques integrating improved light capture, quantum coherence methods of electron transfer and cheap catalytic materials that operate under a variety of atmospheric conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Senior researchers in the field have made the public policy case for a Global Project on Artificial Photosynthesis to address critical energy security and environmental sustainability issues.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
